2023 Innovator of the Year: As AI models are released into the wild, Sharon Li wants to ensure theyâre safe
Liâs research could prevent AI models from failing catastrophically when they encounter unfamiliar scenarios.

By Melissa HeikkilÃ¤archive page
September 12, 2023
Sharon Yixuan Li
SARA STATHAS
Sharon Li is MIT Technology Reviewâs 2023 Innovator of the Year. Meet the rest of this year's Innovators Under 35. 

As we launch AI systems from the lab into the real world, we need to be prepared for these systems to break in surprising and catastrophic ways. Itâs already happening. Last year, for example, a chess-playing robot arm in Moscow fractured the finger of a seven-year-old boy. The robot grabbed the boyâs finger as he was moving a chess piece and let go only after nearby adults managed to pry open its claws. 

Advertisement

This did not happen because the robot was programmed to do harm. It was because the robot was overly confident that the boyâs finger was a chess piece.  

The incident is a classic example of something Sharon Li, 32, wants to prevent. Li, an assistant professor at the University of Wisconsin, Madison, is a pioneer in an AI safety feature called out-of-distribution (OOD) detection. This feature, she says, helps AI models determine when they should abstain from action if faced with something they werenât trained on. 

Li developed one of the first algorithms on out-of-distribution detection for deep neural networks. Google has since set up a dedicated team to integrate OOD detection into its products. Last year, Liâs theoretical analysis of OOD detection was chosen from over 10,000 submissions as an outstanding paper by NeurIPS, one of the most prestigious AI conferences.

Weâre currently in an AI gold rush, and tech companies are racing to release their AI models. But most of todayâs models are trained to identify specific things and often fail when they encounter the unfamiliar scenarios typical of the messy, unpredictable real world. Their inability to reliably understand what they âknowâ and what they donât âknowâ is the weakness behind many AI disasters. 


Sharon Yixuan Li working at her computer
SARA STATHAS
Liâs work calls on the AI community to rethink its approach to training. âA lot of the classic approaches that have been in place over the last 50 years are actually safety unaware,â she says. 

Her approach embraces uncertainty by using machine learning to detect unknown data out in the world and design AI models to adjust to it on the fly. Out-of-distribution detection could help prevent accidents when autonomous cars run into unfamiliar objects on the road, or make medical AI systems more useful in finding a new disease.

âIn all those situations, what we really need [is a] safety-aware machine learning model thatâs able to identify what it doesnât know,â says Li. 

This approach could also aid todayâs buzziest AI technology, large language models such as ChatGPT. These models are often confident liars, presenting falsehoods as facts. This is where OOD detection could help. Say a person asks a chatbot a question it doesnât have an answer to in its training data. Instead of making something up, an AI model using OOD detection would decline to answer.

Liâs research tackles one of the most fundamental questions in machine learning, says John Hopcroft, a professor at Cornell University, who was her PhD advisor. 

Her work has also seen a surge of interest from other researchers. âWhat she is doing is getting other researchers to work,â says Hopcroft, who adds that sheâs âbasically created one of the subfieldsâ of AI safety research.

Now, Li is seeking a deeper understanding of the safety risks relating to large AI models, which are powering all kinds of new online applications and products. She hopes that by making the models underlying these products safer, weâll be better able to mitigate AIâs risks. 

âThe ultimate goal is to ensure trustworthy, safe machine learning,â she says. 

Sharon Li is one of MIT Technology Reviewâs 2023 Innovators Under 35. Meet the rest of this yearâs honorees.

Towards Reliability in Deep Learning Systems
THURSDAY, JULY 14, 2022
Posted by Dustin Tran and Balaji Lakshminarayanan, Research Scientists, Google Research

Deep learning models have made impressive progress in vision, language, and other modalities, particularly with the rise of large-scale pre-training. Such models are most accurate when applied to test data drawn from the same distribution as their training set. However, in practice, the data confronting models in real-world settings rarely match the training distribution. In addition, the models may not be well-suited for applications where predictive performance is only part of the equation. For models to be reliable in deployment, they must be able to accommodate shifts in data distribution and make useful decisions in a broad array of scenarios.

In âPlex: Towards Reliability Using Pre-trained Large Model Extensionsâ, we present a framework for reliable deep learning as a new perspective about a modelâs abilities; this includes a number of concrete tasks and datasets for stress-testing model reliability. We also introduce Plex, a set of pre-trained large model extensions that can be applied to many different architectures. We illustrate the efficacy of Plex in the vision and language domains by applying these extensions to the current state-of-the-art Vision Transformer and T5 models, which results in significant improvement in their reliability. We are also open-sourcing the code to encourage further research into this approach.


Uncertainty â Dog vs. Cat classifier: Plex can say âI donât knowâ for inputs that are neither cat nor dog.
Robust Generalization â A naÃ¯ve model is sensitive to spurious correlations (âdestinationâ), whereas Plex is robust.
Adaptation â Plex can actively choose the data from which it learns to improve performance more quickly.

Framework for Reliability
First, we explore how to understand the reliability of a model in novel scenarios. We posit three general categories of requirements for reliable machine learning (ML) systems: (1) they should accurately report uncertainty about their predictions (âknow what they donât knowâ); (2) they should generalize robustly to new scenarios (distribution shift); and (3) they should be able to efficiently adapt to new data (adaptation). Importantly, a reliable model should aim to do well in all of these areas simultaneously out-of-the-box, without requiring any customization for individual tasks.

Uncertainty reflects the imperfect or unknown information that makes it difficult for a model to make accurate predictions. Predictive uncertainty quantification allows a model to compute optimal decisions and helps practitioners recognize when to trust the modelâs predictions, thereby enabling graceful failures when the model is likely to be wrong.
Robust Generalization involves an estimate or forecast about an unseen event. We investigate four types of out-of-distribution data: covariate shift (when the input distribution changes between training and application and the output distribution is unchanged), semantic (or class) shift, label uncertainty, and subpopulation shift.

Types of distribution shift using an illustration of ImageNet dogs.
Adaptation refers to probing the modelâs abilities over the course of its learning process. Benchmarks typically evaluate on static datasets with pre-defined train-test splits. However, in many applications, we are interested in models that can quickly adapt to new datasets and efficiently learn with as few labeled examples as possible.

Reliability framework. We propose to simultaneously stress-test the âout-of-the-boxâ model performance (i.e., the predictive distribution) across uncertainty, robust generalization, and adaptation benchmarks, without any customization for individual tasks.
We apply 10 types of tasks to capture the three reliability areas â uncertainty, robust generalization, and adaptation â and to ensure that the tasks measure a diverse set of desirable properties in each area. Together the tasks comprise 40 downstream datasets across vision and natural language modalities: 14 datasets for fine-tuning (including few-shot and active learningâbased adaptation) and 26 datasets for out-of-distribution evaluation.


Plex: Pre-trained Large Model Extensions for Vision and Language
To improve reliability, we develop ViT-Plex and T5-Plex, building on large pre-trained models for vision (ViT) and language (T5), respectively. A key feature of Plex is more efficient ensembling based on submodels that each make a prediction that is then aggregated. In addition, Plex swaps each architectureâs linear last layer with a Gaussian process or heteroscedastic layer to better represent predictive uncertainty. These ideas were found to work very well for models trained from scratch at the ImageNet scale. We train the models with varying sizes up to 325 million parameters for vision (ViT-Plex L) and 1 billion parameters for language (T5-Plex L) and pre-training dataset sizes up to 4 billion examples.

The following figure illustrates Plexâs performance on a select set of tasks compared to the existing state-of-the-art. The top-performing model for each task is usually a specialized model that is highly optimized for that problem. Plex achieves new state-of-the-art on many of the 40 datasets. Importantly, Plex achieves strong performance across all tasks using the out-of-the-box model output without requiring any custom designing or tuning for each task.


The largest T5-Plex (top) and ViT-Plex (bottom) models evaluated on a highlighted set of reliability tasks compared to specialized state-of-the-art models. The spokes display different tasks, quantifying metric performance on various datasets.

Plex in Action for Different Reliability Tasks
We highlight Plexâs reliability on select tasks below.


Open Set Recognition
We show Plexâs output in the case where the model must defer prediction because the input is one that the model does not support. This task is known as open set recognition. Here, predictive performance is part of a larger decision-making scenario where the model may abstain from making certain predictions. In the following figure, we show structured open set recognition: Plex returns multiple outputs and signals the specific part of the output about which the model is uncertain and is likely out-of-distribution.


Structured open set recognition enables the model to provide nuanced clarifications. Here, T5-Plex L can recognize fine-grained out-of-distribution cases where the requestâs vertical (i.e., coarse-level domain of service, such as banking, media, productivity, etc.) and domain are supported but the intent is not.

Label Uncertainty
In real-world datasets, there is often inherent ambiguity behind the ground truth label for each input. For example, this may arise due to human rater ambiguity for a given image. In this case, weâd like the model to capture the full distribution of human perceptual uncertainty. We showcase Plex below on examples from an ImageNet variant we constructed that provides a ground truth label distribution.


Plex for label uncertainty. Using a dataset we construct called ImageNet ReaL-H, ViT-Plex L demonstrates the ability to capture the inherent ambiguity (probability distribution) of image labels.

Active Learning
We examine a large modelâs ability to not only learn over a fixed set of data points, but also participate in knowing which data points to learn from in the first place. One such task is known as active learning, where at each training step, the model selects promising inputs among a pool of unlabeled data points on which to train. This procedure assesses an ML modelâs label efficiency, where label annotations may be scarce, and so we would like to maximize performance while minimizing the number of labeled data points used. Plex achieves a significant performance improvement over the same model architecture without pre-training. In addition, even with fewer training examples, it also outperforms the state-of-the-art pre-trained method, BASE, which reaches 63% accuracy at 100K examples.


Active learning on ImageNet1K. ViT-Plex L is highly label efficient compared to a baseline that doesnât leverage pre-training. We also find that active learningâs data acquisition strategy is more effective than uniformly selecting data points at random.

Learn more
Check out our paper here and an upcoming contributed talk about the work at the ICML 2022 pre-training workshop on July 23, 2022. To encourage further research in this direction, we are open-sourcing all code for training and evaluation as part of Uncertainty Baselines. We also provide a demo that shows how to use a ViT-Plex model checkpoint. Layer and method implementations use Edward2.


Acknowledgements
We thank all the co-authors for contributing to the project and paper, including Andreas Kirsch, Clara Huiyi Hu, Du Phan, D. Sculley, Honglin Yuan, Jasper Snoek, Jeremiah Liu, Jie Ren, Joost van Amersfoort, Karan Singhal, Kehang Han, Kelly Buchanan, Kevin Murphy, Mark Collier, Mike Dusenberry, Neil Band, Nithum Thain, Rodolphe Jenatton, Tim G. J. Rudner, Yarin Gal, Zachary Nado, Zelda Mariet, Zi Wang, and Zoubin Ghahramani. We also thank Anusha Ramesh, Ben Adlam, Dilip Krishnan, Ed Chi, Neil Houlsby, Rif A. Saurous, and Sharat Chikkerur for their helpful feedback, and Tom Small and Ajay Nainani for helping with visualizations.

History
Edit

More
Uncertainty quantification (UQ) is the science of quantitative characterization and estimation of uncertainties in both computational and real world applications. It tries to determine how likely certain outcomes are if some aspects of the system are not exactly known. An example would be to predict the acceleration of a human body in a head-on crash with another car: even if the speed was exactly known, small differences in the manufacturing of individual cars, how tightly every bolt has been tightened, etc., will lead to different results that can only be predicted in a statistical sense.

Many problems in the natural sciences and engineering are also rife with sources of uncertainty. Computer experiments on computer simulations are the most common approach to study problems in uncertainty quantification.[1][2][3][41 / 1ChatGPTUncertainty quantification (UQ) focuses on the systematic process of identifying, quantifying, and managing uncertainties in computational and real-world scenarios. In essence, UQ seeks to evaluate how variable outcomes might be when certain elements or variables of a given system aren't known with absolute certainty.The example provided illustrates this concept: even if one were to know the exact speed of two cars involved in a head-on collision, there are numerous factors (e.g., differences in manufacturing, the tightness of bolts, material inconsistencies, etc.) that could influence the outcome, such as the acceleration of a human body upon impact. Given these inherent uncertainties, outcomes can't be predicted deterministically, but rather in a statistical sense, providing a range or distribution of possible outcomes.Furthermore, it's mentioned that uncertainty is not just exclusive to this example. Many real-world problems in natural sciences and engineering come with their sources of uncertainty. Computer simulations or computer experiments have become a popular method to study and address issues related to uncertainty quantification. Through simulations, it's possible to account for various uncertainties and analyze their potential impact on the results, providing a more comprehensive understanding of the system or scenario being studied.1 / 1Sources
Edit
Uncertainty can enter mathematical models and experimental measurements in various contexts. One way to categorize the sources of uncertainty is to consider:[5]

Parameter
This comes from the model parameters that are inputs to the computer model (mathematical model) but whose exact values are unknown to experimentalists and cannot be controlled in physical experiments, or whose values cannot be exactly inferred by statistical methods. Some examples of this are the local free-fall acceleration in a falling object experiment, various material properties in a finite element analysis for engineering, and multiplier uncertainty in the context of macroeconomic policy optimization.
Parametric
This comes from the variability of input variables of the model. For example, the dimensions of a work piece in a process of manufacture may not be exactly as designed and instructed, which would cause variability in its performance.
Structural uncertainty
Also known as model inadequacy, model bias, or model discrepancy, this comes from the lack of knowledge of the underlying physics in the problem. It depends on how accurately a mathematical model describes the true system for a real-life situation, considering the fact that models are almost always only approximations to reality. One example is when modeling the process of a falling object using the free-fall model; the model itself is inaccurate since there always exists air friction. In this case, even if there is no unknown parameter in the model, a discrepancy is still expected between the model and true physics.
Algorithmic
Also known as numerical uncertainty, or discrete uncertainty. This type comes from numerical errors and numerical approximations per implementation of the computer model. Most models are too complicated to solve exactly. For example, the finite element method or finite difference method may be used to approximate the solution of a partial differential equation (which introduces numerical errors). Other examples are numerical integration and infinite sum truncation that are necessary approximations in numerical implementation.
Experimental
Also known as observation error, this comes from the variability of experimental measurements. The experimental uncertainty is inevitable and can be noticed by repeating a measurement for many times using exactly the same settings for all inputs/variables.
Interpolation
This comes from a lack of available data collected from computer model simulations and/or experimental measurements. For other input settings that don't have simulation data or experimental measurements, one must interpolate or extrapolate in order to predict the corresponding responses1 / 1ChatGPTThe sources of uncertainty mentioned here delineate the various ways uncertainty can manifest in mathematical models and experimental measurements. Let's break them down for clarity:Parameter Uncertainty:Arises from unknown model parameters that either cannot be controlled in physical experiments or cannot be precisely determined using statistical methods.Example: The exact value of local free-fall acceleration in a dropping object experiment.Parametric Uncertainty:Concerns the variability of model input variables.Example: A manufactured part might not perfectly match its design dimensions, leading to variability in its function or performance.Structural Uncertainty:Results from an incomplete understanding or approximation of the underlying processes or phenomena.Example: Modeling a free-falling object without considering air resistance introduces structural uncertainty since the real-life process (with air friction) isn't perfectly captured.Algorithmic Uncertainty:Emerges from numerical errors or approximations made when implementing a computer model.Example: Using methods like finite element or finite difference to approximate the solution to a complex equation can introduce errors.Experimental Uncertainty:Originates from variability in experimental measurements. This type of uncertainty is inherent in all measurements and can be observed by repeating the same experiment multiple times.Example: Repeatedly measuring the length of an object with a ruler might yield slightly different results each time.Interpolation Uncertainty:Comes into play when there's missing data, either from computer simulations or physical measurements. Predictions or estimations must be made for these missing data points, introducing uncertainty.Example: If we have data for how a material behaves at 20Â°C and 30Â°C but not at 25Â°C, we must interpolate to estimate its behavior at 25Â°C.By understanding and categorizing these sources of uncertainty, scientists and engineers can better design experiments, develop models, and interpret results, ensuring more robust conclusions and predictions.1 / 1uncertainty quantification a surrogate model, e.g. a Gaussian process or a Polynomial Chaos Expansion, is learnt from computer experiments, this surrogate exhibits epistemic uncertainty that depends on or interacts with the aleatoric uncertainty of the experimental parameters.[4] Such an uncertainty cannot solely be classified as aleatoric or epistemic any more, but is a more general inferential uncertainty.
In real life applications, both kinds of uncertainties are present. Uncertainty quantification intends to explicitly express both types of uncertainty separately. The quantification for the aleatoric uncertainties can be relatively straightforward, where traditional (frequentist) probability is the most basic form. Techniques such as the Monte Carlo method are frequently used. A probability distribution can be represented by its moments (in the Gaussian case, the mean and covariance suffice, although, in general, even knowledge of all moments to arbitrarily high order still does not specify the distribution function uniquely), or more recently, by techniques such as KarhunenâLoÃ¨ve and polynomial chaos expansions. To evaluate epistemic uncertainties, the efforts are made to understand the (lack of) knowledge of the system, process or mechanism. Epistemic uncertainty is generally understood through the lens of Bayesian probability, where probabilities are interpreted as indicating how certain a rational person could be regarding a specific claim.1 / 1ChatGPTUncertainty quantification (UQ) is an essential discipline in computational science and engineering that deals with the characterization and estimation of uncertainties in models, simulations, and real-world applications. The passage you provided delves into the intricacies of uncertainty and its different types, especially when using surrogate models. Let's distill the main points:Types of Uncertainty:Aleatoric Uncertainty: Due to inherent variability in a system. It's typically the "randomness" or "noise" that is inherently present and cannot be reduced even with more data.Epistemic Uncertainty: Stemming from lack of knowledge. It can be reduced with more information or better understanding.Surrogate Models and Uncertainty:When surrogate models like Gaussian processes or Polynomial Chaos Expansions are used, they produce a type of uncertainty that combines both aleatoric and epistemic uncertainties. This combined uncertainty is called "inferential uncertainty".Real-world Uncertainties:In practical scenarios, both aleatoric and epistemic uncertainties exist concurrently. UQ aims to represent both types distinctly.Quantification Methods:Aleatoric Uncertainties: They are often quantified using traditional probability methods. The Monte Carlo method is a standard tool. Representations include probability distribution moments (like mean and covariance) or methods like KarhunenâLoÃ¨ve and polynomial chaos expansions.Epistemic Uncertainties: These uncertainties stem from our lack of understanding or knowledge about a system. The Bayesian approach to probability is typically used to interpret epistemic uncertainty. In this framework, probabilities convey the degree of belief or confidence in a particular claim or outcome based on the available information.In a nutshell, UQ is a complex but essential discipline that helps to differentiate, characterize, and quantify different sources of uncertainties in computational and experimental studies. This ensures that model predictions and decisions based on these models are made with a clear understanding of their associated risks and reliabilities.1 / 1Mathematical perspective
Edit
In mathematics, uncertainty is often characterized in terms of a probability distribution. From that perspective, epistemic uncertainty means not being certain what the relevant probability distribution is, and aleatoric uncertainty means not being certain what a random sample drawn from a probability distribution will be.1 / 1ChatGPTFrom a mathematical standpoint, uncertainty is typically represented using probability theory. Here's a breakdown of your provided content:Probability Distribution: In mathematics, uncertain phenomena are often modeled using probability distributions. This means that instead of having a single predictable outcome, there's a range of possible outcomes, each with its own likelihood.Types of Uncertainty:Epistemic Uncertainty: This uncertainty stems from a lack of knowledge. Mathematically, it's the uncertainty about which probability distribution best represents the uncertain phenomenon. This type of uncertainty can potentially be reduced with more information or data.Aleatoric Uncertainty: This is the inherent randomness in a system. Even if you know the exact probability distribution, there's still uncertainty about the outcome of a specific random sample drawn from that distribution. This type of uncertainty is irreducible and is inherent to the system being observed.In simpler terms, think of epistemic uncertainty as not being sure which dice you're rolling (e.g., a 6-sided die vs. an 8-sided die) and aleatoric uncertainty as not being sure what number will come up when you roll a known dice (e.g., rolling a standard 6-sided die and not knowing if it will land on 1, 2, 3, 4, 5, or 6).1 / 1There are two major types of problems in uncertainty quantification: one is the forward propagation of uncertainty (where the various sources of uncertainty are propagated through the model to predict the overall uncertainty in the system response) and the other is the inverse assessment of model uncertainty and parameter uncertainty (where the model parameters are calibrated simultaneously using test data). There has been a proliferation of research on the former problem and a majority of uncertainty analysis techniques were developed for it. On the other hand, the latter problem is drawing increasing attention in the engineering design community, since uncertainty quantification of a model and the subsequent predictions of the true system response(s) are of great interest in designing robust systems.

Forward
Edit
See also: Propagation of uncertainty
Uncertainty propagation is the quantification of uncertainties in system output(s) propagated from uncertain inputs. It focuses on the influence on the outputs from the parametric variability listed in the sources of uncertainty. The targets of uncertainty propagation analysis can be:

To evaluate low-order moments of the outputs, i.e. mean and variance.
To evaluate the reliability of the outputs. This is especially useful in reliability engineering where outputs of a system are usually closely related to the performance of the system.
To assess the complete probability distribution of the outputs. This is useful in the scenario of utility optimization where the complete distribution is used to calculate the utility.
Inverse
Edit
See also: Inverse problem
Given some experimental measurements of a system and some computer simulation results from its mathematical model, inverse uncertainty quantification estimates the discrepancy between the experiment and the mathematical model (which is called bias correction), and estimates the values of unknown parameters in the model if there are any (which is called parameter calibration or simply calibration). Generally this is a much more difficult problem than forward uncertainty propagation; however it is of great importance since it is typically implemented in a model updating process. There are several scenarios in inverse uncertainty quantification:


The outcome of bias correction, including an updated model (prediction mean) and prediction confidence interval.
Bias correction only
Edit
Bias correction quantifies the model inadequacy, i.e. the discrepancy between the experiment and the mathematical model. The general model updating formula for bias correction is:

{\displaystyle y^{e}(\mathbf {x} )=y^{m}(\mathbf {x} )+\delta (\mathbf {x} )+\varepsilon }y^{e}({\mathbf  {x}})=y^{m}({\mathbf  {x}})+\delta ({\mathbf  {x}})+\varepsilon 
where {\displaystyle y^{e}(\mathbf {x} )}y^{e}({\mathbf  {x}}) denotes the experimental measurements as a function of several input variables {\displaystyle \mathbf {x} }\mathbf {x} , {\displaystyle y^{m}(\mathbf {x} )}y^{m}({\mathbf  {x}}) denotes the computer model (mathematical model) response, {\displaystyle \delta (\mathbf {x} )}\delta ({\mathbf  {x}}) denotes the additive discrepancy function (aka bias function), and {\displaystyle \varepsilon }\varepsilon  denotes the experimental uncertainty. The objective is to estimate the discrepancy function {\displaystyle \delta (\mathbf {x} )}\delta ({\mathbf  {x}}), and as a by-product, the resulting updated model is {\displaystyle y^{m}(\mathbf {x} )+\delta (\mathbf {x} )}y^{m}({\mathbf  {x}})+\delta ({\mathbf  {x}}). A prediction confidence interval is provided with the updated model as the quantification of the uncertainty.

Parameter calibration only
Edit
Parameter calibration estimates the values of one or more unknown parameters in a mathematical model. The general model updating formulation for calibration is:

{\displaystyle y^{e}(\mathbf {x} )=y^{m}(\mathbf {x} ,{\boldsymbol {\theta }}^{*})+\varepsilon }y^{e}({\mathbf  {x}})=y^{m}({\mathbf  {x}},{\boldsymbol  {\theta }}^{*})+\varepsilon 
where {\displaystyle y^{m}(\mathbf {x} ,{\boldsymbol {\theta }})}y^{m}({\mathbf  {x}},{\boldsymbol  {\theta }}) denotes the computer model response that depends on several unknown model parameters {\displaystyle {\boldsymbol {\theta }}}{\boldsymbol  {\theta }}, and {\displaystyle {\boldsymbol {\theta }}^{*}}{\boldsymbol  {\theta }}^{*} denotes the true values of the unknown parameters in the course of experiments. The objective is to either estimate {\displaystyle {\boldsymbol {\theta }}^{*}}{\boldsymbol  {\theta }}^{*}, or to come up with a probability distribution of {\displaystyle {\boldsymbol {\theta }}^{*}}{\boldsymbol  {\theta }}^{*} that encompasses the best knowledge of the true parameter values.

Bias correction and parameter calibration
Edit
It considers an inaccurate model with one or more unknown parameters, and its model updating formulation combines the two together:

{\displaystyle y^{e}(\mathbf {x} )=y^{m}(\mathbf {x} ,{\boldsymbol {\theta }}^{*})+\delta (\mathbf {x} )+\varepsilon }y^{e}({\mathbf  {x}})=y^{m}({\mathbf  {x}},{\boldsymbol  {\theta }}^{*})+\delta ({\mathbf  {x}})+\varepsilon 
It is the most comprehensive model updating formulation that includes all possible sources of uncertainty, and it requires the most effort to solve.1 / 1ChatGPTIn uncertainty quantification, there are two main types of problems: forward propagation of uncertainty and inverse assessment. Here's a simplified summary of what you provided:Forward Propagation of Uncertainty: This involves taking known uncertainties from input parameters and understanding how they affect the outcomes (outputs).Purpose:Calculate basic statistics of the outputs (like mean and variance).Understand the reliability of these outputs, especially if they're tied to system performance.Get the complete probability distribution of the outputs, helpful for optimization scenarios.Inverse Assessment: Here, you have some real-world data (experimental measurements) and some results from a computer simulation. The goal is to align the simulation with reality by adjusting the model. This involves:Bias Correction Only: This quantifies the discrepancy between the real-world data and the model. The aim is to identify and correct this bias so the model is more accurate.Parameter Calibration Only: This adjusts unknown parameters in the model to make it fit experimental data better.Bias Correction and Parameter Calibration: This is a comprehensive approach. If a model has unknown parameters and is also slightly inaccurate, this method adjusts the model to fit the real-world data, considering both bias and parameter uncertainties.To visualize it simply:Forward propagation is like throwing a stone into a pond and predicting where the ripples will go based on how you threw it.Inverse assessment is like seeing ripples in a pond and trying to determine how the stone was thrown.Both types of problems are crucial in the engineering and scientific community. While much research has been done on forward problems, the inverse assessment is gaining traction because it's essential for creating reliable and robust systems.1 / 1
Selective methodologies
Edit
Much research has been done to solve uncertainty quantification problems, though a majority of them deal with uncertainty propagation. During the past one to two decades, a number of approaches for inverse uncertainty quantification problems have also been developed and have proved to be useful for most small- to medium-scale problems.

Forward propagation
Edit
Existing uncertainty propagation approaches include probabilistic approaches and non-probabilistic approaches. There are basically six categories of probabilistic approaches for uncertainty propagation:[9]

Simulation-based methods: Monte Carlo simulations, importance sampling, adaptive sampling, etc.
General surrogate-based methods: In a non-instrusive approach, a surrogate model is learnt in order to replace the experiment or the simulation with a cheap and fast approximation. Surrogate-based methods can also be employed in a fully Bayesian fashion. [10][4][11][12] This approach has proven particularly powerful when the cost of sampling, e.g. computationally expensive simulations, is prohibitively high.
Local expansion-based methods: Taylor series, perturbation method, etc. These methods have advantages when dealing with relatively small input variability and outputs that don't express high nonlinearity. These linear or linearized methods are detailed in the article Uncertainty propagation.
Functional expansion-based methods: Neumann expansion, orthogonal or KarhunenâLoeve expansions (KLE), with polynomial chaos expansion (PCE) and wavelet expansions as special cases.
Most probable point (MPP)-based methods: first-order reliability method (FORM) and second-order reliability method (SORM).
Numerical integration-based methods: Full factorial numerical integration (FFNI) and dimension reduction (DR).
For non-probabilistic approaches, interval analysis,[13] Fuzzy theory, Possibility theory and evidence theory are among the most widely used.

The probabilistic approach is considered as the most rigorous approach to uncertainty analysis in engineering design due to its consistency with the theory of decision analysis. Its cornerstone is the calculation of probability density functions for sampling statistics.[14] This can be performed rigorously for random variables that are obtainable as transformations of Gaussian variables, leading to exact confidence intervals1 / 1ChatGPTSelective Methodologies for Uncertainty QuantificationWhen dealing with uncertainty in scientific and engineering contexts, various methods have been developed to quantify and understand it. The focus has largely been on forward propagation, though inverse uncertainty methods have also gained traction. Here's a condensed overview:Forward Propagation Methods:Probabilistic Approaches:Simulation-based methods: Techniques like Monte Carlo simulations, importance sampling, and adaptive sampling are used. These are essentially experiments run multiple times to understand the spread and impact of uncertainty.General surrogate-based methods: Instead of directly running a potentially costly or time-consuming experiment or simulation, a surrogate model is created. This is a simplified representation that can be "queried" much more quickly. When data collection is expensive or slow, surrogate models are invaluable.Local expansion-based methods: These include methods like the Taylor series or perturbation method. They're good for scenarios where the input variability is small and outputs are not highly nonlinear.Functional expansion-based methods: Techniques such as Neumann expansion or orthogonal expansions like the KarhunenâLoÃ¨ve expansions. Special cases include polynomial chaos expansion and wavelet expansions.Most probable point (MPP)-based methods: This includes the first-order reliability method (FORM) and the second-order reliability method (SORM), which focus on understanding the most likely scenarios.Numerical integration-based methods: These involve methods like Full factorial numerical integration (FFNI) and dimension reduction (DR) which mathematically integrate over the sources of uncertainty.Non-Probabilistic Approaches:Interval Analysis: Instead of giving a specific value, you give a range (or interval) where the value can lie.Fuzzy Theory: This approach deals with possibilities rather than exact probabilities. For instance, the possibility that an event is "somewhat likely" or "very unlikely".Possibility Theory: Similar to fuzzy theory, but often used for a different set of problems.Evidence Theory: This method is used when evidence or information is combined from various sources to determine uncertainty.The probabilistic approach is often viewed as the most rigorous for uncertainty analysis in engineering design. It aligns well with decision analysis theories and focuses on the calculation of probability density functions, leading to exact confidence intervals when variables transform from Gaussian distributions.1 / 1hyperparameters in previous modules.

Module 4: Prediction of the experimental response and discrepancy function
Full approach
Edit
Fully Bayesian approach requires that not only the priors for unknown parameters {\displaystyle {\boldsymbol {\theta }}}{\boldsymbol  {\theta }} but also the priors for the other hyperparameters {\displaystyle {\boldsymbol {\varphi }}}{\displaystyle {\boldsymbol {\varphi }}} should be assigned. It follows the following steps:[16]

Derive the posterior distribution {\displaystyle p({\boldsymbol {\theta }},{\boldsymbol {\varphi }}\mid {\text{data}})}{\displaystyle p({\boldsymbol {\theta }},{\boldsymbol {\varphi }}\mid {\text{data}})};
Integrate {\displaystyle {\boldsymbol {\varphi }}}{\displaystyle {\boldsymbol {\varphi }}} out and obtain {\displaystyle p({\boldsymbol {\theta }}\mid {\text{data}})}{\displaystyle p({\boldsymbol {\theta }}\mid {\text{data}})}. This single step accomplishes the calibration;
Prediction of the experimental response and discrepancy function.
However, the approach has significant drawbacks:

For most cases, {\displaystyle p({\boldsymbol {\theta }},{\boldsymbol {\varphi }}\mid {\text{data}})}{\displaystyle p({\boldsymbol {\theta }},{\boldsymbol {\varphi }}\mid {\text{data}})} is a highly intractable function of {\displaystyle {\boldsymbol {\varphi }}}{\displaystyle {\boldsymbol {\varphi }}}. Hence the integration becomes very troublesome. Moreover, if priors for the other hyperparameters {\displaystyle {\boldsymbol {\varphi }}}{\displaystyle {\boldsymbol {\varphi }}} are not carefully chosen, the complexity in numerical integration increases even more.
In the prediction stage, the prediction (which should at least include the expected value of system responses) also requires numerical integration. Markov chain Monte Carlo (MCMC) is often used for integration; however it is computationally expensive.
The fully Bayesian approach requires a huge amount of calculations and may not yet be practical for dealing with the most complicated modelling situations.[16]1 / 1ChatGPTModule 4: Prediction of the Experimental Response and Discrepancy Function - Full ApproachFully Bayesian Approach:In Bayesian statistics, the approach is based on updating prior beliefs (priors) with new evidence (data) to obtain more informed posterior beliefs (posteriors). In the context of uncertainty quantification, this approach involves not just the unknown parameters, Î¸{\boldsymbol {\theta }}Î¸, but also the hyperparameters, Ï{\boldsymbol {\varphi }}Ï. Here's a summary of the process:Derive the Posterior Distribution: The joint posterior distribution, p(Î¸,Ïâ£data)p({\boldsymbol {\theta }},{\boldsymbol {\varphi }}| {\text{data}})p(Î¸,Ïâ£data), represents the updated belief about the parameters and hyperparameters after observing the data.Integration of Hyperparameters: The hyperparameters, Ï{\boldsymbol {\varphi }}Ï, are integrated out to obtain the posterior distribution of the parameters, p(Î¸â£data)p({\boldsymbol {\theta }}| {\text{data}})p(Î¸â£data). This step accomplishes the calibration of the unknown parameters.Prediction: Once the parameters are calibrated, predictions of the experimental response and discrepancy function can be made.Challenges with the Fully Bayesian Approach:Intractability: The joint posterior distribution, p(Î¸,Ïâ£data)p({\boldsymbol {\theta }},{\boldsymbol {\varphi }}| {\text{data}})p(Î¸,Ïâ£data), is often complex and difficult to handle, especially as a function of Ï{\boldsymbol {\varphi }}Ï. This makes the integration step challenging. If the priors for the hyperparameters are not well-defined, the numerical integration becomes even more complex.Computational Expenses in Prediction: Predictions, especially those involving expected values of system responses, require additional numerical integration. Techniques like Markov chain Monte Carlo (MCMC) are common, but they're computationally demanding.Practicality: Given the computational challenges and the need for a high level of calculations, the fully Bayesian approach might not be feasible for highly complex models or situations.In essence, while the Bayesian approach is theoretically sound and can provide comprehensive insights, its practical implementation in highly complex scenarios is challenging due to computational demands and intricacies associated with hyperparameters.1 / 1Could you give some simplified everyday life examples?1 / 1ChatGPTCertainly! Energy-based Out-of-distribution Detection
Weitang Liu, Xiaoyun Wang, John D. Owens, Yixuan Li
Determining whether inputs are out-of-distribution (OOD) is an essential building block for safely deploying machine learning models in the open world. However, previous methods relying on the softmax confidence score suffer from overconfident posterior distributions for OOD data. We propose a unified framework for OOD detection that uses an energy score. We show that energy scores better distinguish in- and out-of-distribution samples than the traditional approach using the softmax scores. Unlike softmax confidence scores, energy scores are theoretically aligned with the probability density of the inputs and are less susceptible to the overconfidence issue. Within this framework, energy can be flexibly used as a scoring function for any pre-trained neural classifier as well as a trainable cost function to shape the energy surface explicitly for OOD detection. On a CIFAR-10 pre-trained WideResNet, using the energy score reduces the average FPR (at TPR 95%) by 18.03% compared to the softmax confidence score. With energy-based training, our method outperforms the state-of-the-art on common benchmarks.1 / 1ChatGPTThis is an abstract for a paper titled "Energy-based Out-of-distribution Detection" by Weitang Liu, Xiaoyun Wang, John D. Owens, and Yixuan Li. Let's break down the main points:Purpose: The paper addresses the challenge of determining if input data is out-of-distribution (OOD), which is crucial for the safe deployment of machine learning models in real-world scenarios.Issue with Previous Methods: Traditional methods that rely on softmax confidence scores tend to produce overconfident posterior distributions when dealing with OOD data. This can be problematic, leading to false assurances about the model's performance on unfamiliar data.Proposed Solution: The authors introduce a unified framework for OOD detection that leverages an "energy score". This score is posited to be more effective than softmax scores at differentiating between in-distribution and OOD samples.Advantages of Energy Scores: Unlike softmax scores, which can become overconfident, energy scores are theoretically linked with the probability density of the inputs. This makes them less prone to the overconfidence problem.Flexibility: The energy score is versatile and can be used as both a scoring function for pre-existing neural classifiers and a trainable cost function. This means it can be integrated with existing systems or used to shape a new model's energy surface specifically for OOD detection.Performance Metrics: The paper presents empirical results showing the efficacy of the energy score. For instance, on a model trained on the CIFAR-10 dataset (using WideResNet), the energy score lowered the average False Positive Rate (FPR) by 18.03% (when True Positive Rate (TPR) was 95%) in comparison to using the softmax confidence score.Comparison with State-of-the-Art: The proposed method with energy-based training outperformed existing state-of-the-art methods on widely recognized benchmarks.In essence, this paper highlights the potential benefits of using energy scores over traditional softmax confidence scores for detecting OOD inputs, aiming to enhance the safety and reliability of deploying machine learning models in diverse environments.1 / 1No Pseudolikelihood for Me: Training Potts Models with Contrastive Divergence for Protein Design
The current 250 million known protein sequences are the result of the generative process evolution. Similar sequences that have likely evolved from the same ancestral sequence, also known as homologs, are clustered in families stored in the Pfam database. Potts models trained on a Pfam family can predict a common protein 3D structure to the sequences in that family, predict the beneficial or detrimental effect of mutations, and recently be sampled to generate novel sequences for protein design.

A protein is a string of amino acids, also called residues, that in cells can adopt a 3-dimensional fold. These folds mean that two residues far apart in the sequence can be nearby in 3D space. One assumption underlying all protein science is that proteins structures prefer to adopt low energy states. Interactions with neighbouring atoms should be locally favourable. Thus, for mutations to not disrupt protein folds, residues nearby in 3D space tend to coevolve, i.e. a residueâs evolutionary trajectory is dependent on nearby residues. Potts models are attractive for protein modelling because of its inductive bias. In particular the pairwise term can capture coevolutionary information in sequences.1 / 1ChatGPTThis seems to be an introduction or abstract for a paper or article related to using Potts models for protein design. These families are stored in the Pfam database.Importance of Potts Models: These models, when trained on a specific Pfam family, can:Predict a common 3D structure shared by the sequences within that family.Foresee whether certain mutations will be beneficial or harmful.Be sampled to produce new sequences, which is beneficial for designing proteins.Proteins Defined: Proteins are chains of amino acids (referred to as residues). While these residues are linear in sequence, within a cell, they can fold into complex 3D structures.Significance of Folds: Even if two residues are distant from each other in the sequence, they can be close in the 3D spatial arrangement. The way proteins fold is significant to their function.Underlying Assumption: All protein science is based on the assumption that protein structures gravitate towards low energy states. This means they adopt shapes and configurations that are energetically favorable. The atoms interact in a way that's beneficial locally.Coevolution in Proteins: Residues that are in proximity within the 3D space tend to evolve in a coordinated manner. This means that if one residue changes or mutates, it can influence the evolution of neighboring residues.Why Potts Models?: Potts models are especially suitable for protein modeling. Their structure, particularly the pairwise term, can detect and encapsulate the coevolutionary information present in sequences.In summary, this piece emphasizes the potential of Potts models in understanding and predicting protein structures and evolution, especially given their capability to capture coevolutionary patterns in protein sequences.1 / 1It was at https://github.com/HussainAther/potts1 / 1ChatGPTI'm unable to access specific URLs or external databases, such as GitHub, directly. Subham Sahoo, Christoph Lampert, Georg Martius Proceedings of the 35th International Conference on Machine Learning, PMLR 80:4442-4450, 2018.
Abstract
We present an approach to identify concise equations from data using a shallow neural network approach. In contrast to ordinary black-box regression, this approach allows understanding functional relations and generalizing them from observed data to unseen parts of the parameter space. We show how to extend the class of learnable equations for a recently proposed equation learning network to include divisions, and we improve the learning and model selection strategy to be useful for challenging real-world data. For systems governed by analytical expressions, our method can in many cases identify the true underlying equation and extrapolate to unseen domains. We demonstrate its effectiveness by experiments on a cart-pendulum system, where only 2 random rollouts are required to learn the forward dynamics and successfully achieve the swing-up task.1 / 1ChatGPTThis paper, "Learning Equations for Extrapolation and Control" by Subham Sahoo, Christoph Lampert, and Georg Martius, delves into the realm of extracting structured knowledge from data. This has the advantage of understanding and extrapolating these relationships to parts of the parameter space that have not been observed.Key Contributions:Expanding Learnable Equations: The authors expand the class of equations that a recently suggested equation learning network can learn. Introduction 
In machine learning, models are typically treated as black- 
box function approximators that are only judged by their 
ability to predict correctly for unseen data (from the same 
distribution).In contrast, in the natural sciences, one 
searches for interpretable models that provide a deeper un- 
derstanding of the system of interest and allow formulating 
hypotheses about unseen situations. The latter is only pos- 
sible if the true underlying functional relationship behind 
the data has been identif i ed. Therefore, when scientists con- 
struct models, they do not only minimize a training error but 
also impose constraints based on prior knowledge: models 
should be plausible, i.e. consist of components that have 
physical expressions in the real world, and they should be 
interpretable, which typically means that they consist only 
of a small number of interacting units.

Lampert (2016) recently proposed EQL, a neural network 
architecture for identifying functional relations between ob- 
served inputs and outputs. Their networks represent only 
plausible functions through a specif i c choice of activation 
functions and it prefers simple over complex solutions by 
imposing sparsity regularization. However, EQL has two 
signif i cant shortcomings: i rst, it is not able to represent di- 
visions, thereby severely limiting to which physical systems 
it can be applied, and second, its model selection procedure 
is unreliable in identifying the true functional relation out 
of multiple plausible candidates. 
In this paper, we propose an improved network for 
equation learning, EQLÃ·, that overcomes the limitation 
of the earlier works. In particular, our main contributions are 
1.we propose a network architecture that can handle di- 
visions as well as techniques to keep training stable, 
2.we improve model/instance selection to be more effec- 
tive in identifying the right network/equation, 
3.we demonstrate how to reliably control a dynamical 
robotic system by learning its forward dynamics equa- 
tions from very few random tryouts/tails. 
The following section describes the equation learning 
method by Martius & Lampert (2016) and introduces our 
improvements. Afterwards, we discuss its relation to other 
prior work. In Section 4 we present experimental results 
on identifying equations and in Section 5 we show its ap- 
plication to robot control. We close with a discussion and 
outlook.

2.2. Introducing division units 
The EQL architecture has some immediate shortcomings. 
In particular, it cannot model divisions, which are, however, 
common in the equations governing physical systems. We, 
therefore, propose a new architecture, EQLÃ·, that includes 
division units, which calculatea/b. Note that this is a non- 
trivial step because any division creates a pole atb â 0with 
an abrupt change in convexity and diverging function value 
and its derivative. Such a divergence is a serious problem 
for gradient based optimization methods. 
To overcome the divergence problem, we i rst notice that 
from any real system we cannot encounter data at the pole 
itself because natural quantities do not diverge. This im- 
plies that a single branch of the hyperbola1/bwithb > 0 
suff i ces as a basis function. As a further simplif i cation we 
use divisions only in the ouput layer. 
Finally, in order to prevent problems during optimization 
we introduce a curriculum approach for optimization, pro- 
gressing from a strongly regularized version of division to 
the unregularized one. 
Regularized Division:Instead of EQLâs Eq.(1), the last 
layer of the EQLÃ·is

Here's a breakdown:2.1. Equation Learner (EQL):EQL Basics: EQL is a multi-layer feed-forward network where each unit represents a specific algebraic operation such as identity, cosine, or multiplication. It's not just a homogeneous network but has specific roles for each of its units.Functionality: EQL uses linear transformations and applies base-functions on them. It has:Unary functions like identity, sine, and cosine.Binary functions which are multiplication units that calculate the product of two inputs.Output Computation: The final layer computes the regression values through a linear transformation.2.2. Introducing division units:Need for Division: The original EQL couldn't model divisions, which are crucial for equations in physical systems.Challenges with Division: Directly implementing division poses a challenge due to the divergence problem, i.e., dividing by near-zero values can lead to very large outputs and thus introduce instability in gradient-based optimization.Solution: The authors note that in real systems, data never really reaches the point of causing a pole (i.e., division by zero). They also restrict the use of division to the output layer only, which simplifies the architecture.Regularized Division: To tackle the issues with raw division, the authors introduce a division-activation function that only divides when the denominator is above a certain threshold. This way, they avoid issues tied to very small denominators.Penalty Term: To further ensure stability, a penalty term is added to the cost function to discourage the network from using denominators that are too small. This penalty term linearly increases for function values below the desired threshold.Figure 2 Explanation: The figure visualizes the regularized division function and the associated penalty term. It showcases how the penalty term increases for function values below the chosen threshold.In essence, this section details the significant enhancement of the original EQL by introducing the capability to handle division operations, while ensuring stability and avoiding pitfalls associated with such operations. RegularizationPhases:We follow the same regularization 
scheme as proposed in Martius & Lampert (2016). The 
role of the regularization is to create a network with sparse 
connections, corresponding to the few terms appearing in 
a typical formula describing a physical system. Ideally, we 
would like to minimize theL0norm directly, but this would 
make the loss not differentiable. As commonly done, we use 
theL1norm as a substitute. However, as detailed below, we 
add a step for emulating a trade-off free L0minimization. 
The regularization scheme is as follows: we start with an 
un-regularized phase (Î» = 0) because starting withL1reg- 
ularization sometimes causes weights not to change sign 
during training but getting âtrappedâ at zero. We continue 
by a phase, where regularization is normally enabled by 
settingÎ»to a nonzero value, which has the effect that a 
sparse network structure emerges. Such a phasing was also 
suggest by Carpenter (2008). As a side remark, subgradient 
methods or clipping (Schmidt et al., 2009; Carpenter, 2008) 
was not required to deal with theL1regularizer because 
potentially slight weight l uctuations around zero are not 
important due to the next phase which is motivated by the 
following fact. A non-zeroL1regularization term leads to a 
trade-off between minimizing the loss and the regularizer. 
Since our aim is to learn the right functional form with the 
correct coeff i cients, we add a third phase where we disable 
L1regularization (Î» = 0) but enforce the sameL0norm 
of the weights. This is achieved by keeping all weights 
w â W1...Lthat 
are close to0at0, i.e. if|w| < 0.001then 
w = 0during the remaining epochs. In this way, the model 
complexity is i xed and we ensure that function values i t 
the observed values as closely as possible, allowing for a 
correct parameter estimation. It also eliminates any poten- 
tial l uctuations of small weights that might occur during 
theL1phase. The effect of the regularization phases is 
schematically illustrated in Fig. 3.

Here's a summary:Penalty Epochs:Objective: Ensure that the trained model doesn't produce negative denominators, even for extrapolation data. They also want to guard against overfitting.Implementation: Every 50 epochs, they introduce a "penalty epoch" where they sample input data points in the expected test and extrapolation range. The loss during these epochs includes both the penalty for small/negative denominators and a boundary penalty for output values that deviate too much from the expected range.Curriculum:The threshold Î¸ in the division function helps avoid large gradients during training.Over the course of training, this threshold is gradually reduced, making the model more precise over time.For testing and validation, a very small threshold is used.2.3. Network Training:Model Parameters: EQLÃ· is trained end-to-end using back-propagation, optimizing over the free parameters.Objective Function: They use a Lasso-like loss, which is a combination of L2 loss and L1 regularization, and also include the penalty for small/negative denominators. The boundary penalty is only used during penalty epochs.Optimization: They utilize stochastic gradient descent with mini-batches and employ the Adam optimizer. However, they note that even standard SGD can work, albeit potentially slower.Regularization Phases:Purpose: Achieve a sparse network, which means fewer active connections. This reflects real-world equations that typically have fewer terms.Phase 1 (No Regularization): They start without L1 regularization to prevent weights from getting stuck at zero.Phase 2 (L1 Regularization): Regularization is introduced to produce a sparse network. This is common practice in many machine learning models.Phase 3 (Emulating L0 Regularization): The final phase removes the L1 regularization and enforces a fixed L0 norm by setting small weights to zero. This ensures the model fits the observed data closely and gives a more accurate parameter estimation.Timing: The authors specify certain times (t1 and t2) to transition between these phases, based on a fraction of the total number of training epochs.Figure 3: Illustrates these regularization phases. In the first phase, weights move freely without regularization. In the second, many weights are set to zero due to L1 regularization. In the third and final phase, weights close to zero remain zero, while others adjust to their correct value.In summary, this segment sheds light on the training intricacies of the EQLÃ· network, highlighting the importance of regularization and penalty epochs in achieving a well-fitted, robust model. The phased regularization approach is particularly noteworthy as it aims to capture the essence of real-world physical equations, which are generally sparse in nature.1 / 1Here are the key points about model selection for extrapolation based on the passage:

- Model selection is critical for good extrapolation capabilities. The goal is to select the "right" formula/model that generalizes well.

- Two cases are considered - with or without extrapolation data for model selection. 

- Without extrapolation data, selection is based on validation error and sparsity/simplicity of the model. This is called the Vint-S selection method. 

- With some extrapolation data (e.g. 40 points), an extra validation error term vex is introduced. This is called the Vint&ex selection method. 

- Sparsity becomes less important when vex is considered, as counting nodes may not properly capture simplicity for extrapolation. 

- Relatively few extrapolation points (e.g. 40) are sufficient to identify the right model among many candidates, due to large errors differences for correct vs incorrect formulas.

- Multiple runs are used to quantify expected performance deviations, as the optimization problem is non-convex.

So in summary, the key aspects of model selection discussed are using validation error, sparsity, and extrapolation error terms depending on available data, to pick the "right" or best generalizing formula according to Occam's razor. Sparsity is de-emphasized when extrapolation data is available.1 / 1ChatGPTAbsolutely, you've summarized the key points regarding model selection for extrapolation succinctly. To reiterate:Extrapolation Importance: Selecting the right model/formula is crucial for effective extrapolation, ensuring the model not only fits the data it was trained on but can also predict accurately on new, unseen data.Two Selection Scenarios:Without extrapolation data: The model is chosen based on validation error combined with the sparsity or simplicity of the model (Vint-S method).With extrapolation data: A term for extrapolation error, termed vex, is introduced and considered in the selection process (Vint&ex method).Role of Sparsity:When no extrapolation data is available, sparsity becomes a key metric to capture the simplicity of the model.However, with extrapolation data, the importance of sparsity reduces. This implies that simply counting nodes or elements in a model doesn't necessarily indicate its effectiveness in extrapolating.Extrapolation Data: Even a relatively small amount of extrapolation data can significantly aid in choosing the right model. The reason being, the difference in errors between correct and incorrect formulas becomes glaringly obvious with this data.Non-Convexity: Due to the non-convex nature of the optimization problem, multiple runs are carried out to account for potential variations in model performance.In essence, model selection for extrapolation, as discussed, is a nuanced process. It strikes a balance between fitting the available data and ensuring generalizability, underlined by the principles of Occam's razor.1 / 1The Vint-S selection method is used when there is no extrapolation data available for model selection. Here are some more details:

- It relies on the validation error (vint) and sparsity (s) of each model/instance. 

- The validation error and sparsity are normalized across all instances to values between 0 and 1. This is denoted as vint(Ï) and s(Ï).

- The criterion used to select the best model is to minimize a weighted sum of the normalized validation error and sparsity:

argmin Ï (Î± * vint(Ï)^2 + Î² * s(Ï)^2)

- Î± and Î² are weighting factors that are empirically determined, with Î± = 0.5 and Î² = 0.5 used. 

- This finds a balance between low validation error (want to minimize) and low sparsity/high simplicity (also want to minimize).

- It considers both quantities together, rather than just comparing ranks, to be more sensitive to numeric differences between instances.

- The instance Ï that minimizes this weighted criterion is selected as the "best" model for extrapolation using only the available validation data.

So in summary, it normalizes and combines validation error and sparsity metrics to quantitatively evaluate models when no extrapolation data is available for selection.1 / 1ChatGPTYou've articulated the Vint-S selection method quite comprehensively. Let's recap:Applicability: The Vint-S method is employed when there's an absence of extrapolation data.Key Metrics:Validation Error (vint): The difference between the model's predicted outputs and the actual outputs on validation data.Sparsity (s): A measure of the simplicity of the model, where a sparser model indicates greater simplicity.Normalization: Both vint and s metrics for each model instance are normalized to range between 0 and 1, ensuring a consistent scale for comparison.Selection Criterion: The best model, denoted by Ï, is chosen by minimizing the weighted sum of the squared normalized validation error and sparsity:argmin Ï(Î±Ãvint(Ï)2+Î²Ãs(Ï)2)\text{argmin } \psi \left( \alpha \times \text{vint}(\psi)^2 + \beta \times \text{s}(\psi)^2 \right)argmin Ï(Î±Ãvint(Ï)2+Î²Ãs(Ï)2)Where Î± and Î² are weights to determine the relative importance of validation error and sparsity, respectively.Empirical Weights: Î± = 0.5 and Î² = 0.5 are used, emphasizing an equal balance between minimizing validation error and sparsity.Rationale: By considering both metrics in a unified criterion, the method can more sensitively discern numerical differences between model instances, rather than relying on simplistic rank comparisons.End Goal: The model instance Ï, which results in the lowest value for the combined metric, is selected as the optimal one for extrapolation based on available validation data.In essence, the Vint-S method offers a systematic way to evaluate and select the most suitable model for extrapolation when only validation data is at hand, leveraging both error and simplicity metrics.1 / 13. Relation to prior work 
In this work, we are departing from the classical path in 
machine learning of i nding any function that yields a small 
expected error on future data of the same distribution as the 
training data. Instead, we aim at discovering the underlying 
relationship between input and output, much like it is done 
in the natural sciences. In this way an interpretable function 
Learning Equations for Extrapolation and Control 
with concise form is obtained. 
In machine learning, this task has received little attention 
but is studied in the i eld of system identif i cation. The meth- 
ods from system identif i cation assume that the functional 
form of the system is known and only the parameters have 
to be identif i ed. Recently, this was shown to be effective 
for identif i ying partial differential equations in a range of 
systems (Rudy et al., 2017). In our work, we also learn 
the parameters of the base functions and, most importantly, 
their composition. 
The task of i nding equations for observations is also known 
as symbolic regression.For a given function space, a 
heuristic search is performed, typically with evolutionary 
computation. With these techniques, it is possible to dis- 
cover physical laws such as invariants and conserved quan- 
tities (Schmidt & Lipson, 2009). Unfortunately, due to 
the exponential search space, the computational complex- 
ity becomes prohibitive for larger expressions and high- 
dimensional problems. We attempt to circumvent this by 
modeling it as a gradient-based optimization problem. Re- 
lated to symbolic regression is i nding mathematical iden- 
tities for instance to i nd computationally more eff i cient 
expressions. In (Zaremba et al., 2014), this was done using 
machine learning to overcome the potentially exponential 
search space. 
Another relation to our work is in causal learning, which 
aims at identifying a causal relationship between multiple 
observables, originating from some underlying functional 
mechanism. This was pioneered by Pearl (2000) who re- 
duces this to i nding a minimal graphical model based only 
on tests of conditional independence. Although it provides 
a factorization of the problem and reveals causes and ef- 
fects, it leaves the exact functional dependency unexplained. 
In order to reveal causal relationships from fewer observ- 
ables (e.g. just two), in Peters et al. (2014) a functional 
view was taken. However, the causal inference is based 
on the expected noise distribution instead of the simplic- 
ity/plausibility of regression functions. 
Our work has some similarity to domain adaptation, because 
data from the extrapolation domain differs from the data 
distribution at training time. As we assume a shared labeling 
function between both, our situation i ts the covariate shift 
(Quionero-Candela et al., 2009) setting. However, existing 
domain adaptation techniques, such as sample reweighting 
(Sugiyama et al., 2007) are not applicable, because training 
and extrapolation domain are disjoint for us. Where existing 
approaches rely on an assumption of distribution similarity, 
we instead make use of the fact that the target function has 
an analytic, and therefore global characterization. For the 
same reasons, existing theoretic results, such as Ben-David 
et al. (2010) are not applicable (or vacuous) in our setting. 
For a discussion on the architectural relation to prior work 
we refer to Martius & Lampert (2016). To summarize, 
the individual components, such as product units or sine 
units were introduced before. However, the combination as 
introduced here and the restriction to pairwise multiplication 
terms are new. To the best of our knowledge, we are the i rst 
to use division units in a neural network.1 / 1ChatGPTThis section outlines the relation of the presented work to prior studies in the field. Let's summarize the main points:Departure from Classical Machine Learning:The research deviates from the traditional machine learning goal of finding a function with minimal future error based on similar training data.The objective is to determine the relationship between input and output, resembling methods used in natural sciences.Interpretable Function Learning:The focus is on obtaining interpretable functions with clear and concise forms.System Identification:While traditional machine learning has not prioritized this task, system identification has.Conventional system identification methods presume knowledge of the system's functional form, focusing on parameter identification.The study cites Rudy et al. (2017) which effectively identifies partial differential equations for systems.Unlike prior works, the research also identifies base function parameters and their composition.Symbolic Regression:The task involves finding equations for observations.Typically, a heuristic search in the function space is performed, often via evolutionary computation.This approach can uncover physical laws, but struggles with computational complexity in expansive search spaces.The research addresses this challenge by modeling it as a gradient-based optimization problem.Mathematical Identities:Zaremba et al. (2014) used machine learning to efficiently discover mathematical identities, sidestepping the expansive search space.Causal Learning:This aims to discern causal relations between observables.Pearl (2000) proposed a method that reduces it to finding a minimal graphical model based on conditional independence tests.Peters et al. (2014) introduced a functional view but relied on expected noise distribution rather than regression function simplicity.Domain Adaptation & Covariate Shift:The research has parallels with domain adaptation since extrapolation domain data differs from training data.The situation aligns with the covariate shift setting, as per Quionero-Candela et al. (2009).Traditional domain adaptation methods aren't suitable because training and extrapolation domains are separate.The research exploits the fact that the target function possesses an analytical, global characterization.Architectural Relation:For more detailed architectural relations, readers are directed to Martius & Lampert (2016).The work introduces novel components in neural networks, such as division units, a first-of-its-kind according to the authors.In essence, this research builds upon and extends various existing methods to present a novel approach to function learning and extrapolation, offering an interpretable and analytical relationship between inputs and outputs.1 / 14. 4.3. Random expressions 
In order to avoid a bias through hand-crafted formulas, we 
generated random functional expressions. The expressions 
were generated with our architecture with2and3hidden 
Learning Equations for Extrapolation and Control 
layers and random sparse connections,4instances each, 
named as RE{2,3}-{1,2,3,4}. 
The weights and biases 
were sampled from a uniform distribution in[0.5,2]and 
were multiplied with[1,â1]with 
equal probability. Further, 
the input weights into thesinandcosnodes were multi- 
plied byÏ, to avoid including only the linear regime of the 
trigonometric functions. Some of the expressions are very 
hard to learn, even in the interpolation region. Table 2 shows 
the experimental results. We also compare the two model- 
selection strategies (Vint-S and Vint&ex). It becomes evident 
that without a few points from the extrapolation region (here 
40) the system is not able to identify the right formula in the 
majority of cases. For the baselines (MLP, SVR) the model 
selection based on extrapolation only reduced the strong 
outliers but did not yield acceptable performance. This is 
expected because these methods have no chance to identify 
the right functional relationship. Figure 6(b) illustrates the 
complicated structure at the example of RE3-4. Again, note 
that the extrapolation domain is 15 times larger than the 
training domain. 
4.4. Cart-pendulum system 
Let us now consider a non-trivial physical system: a pendu-

the cart, the velocity of the cart, the angle of the pendulum 
and the angular velocity of the pendulum. We combine 
these into a four-dimensional vectorx = (x1,...,x4). We

At the beginning no knowledge about the system is avail-

C = 0.1x2+ 0.1Ë x2â cos(Î¸) + 0.02ËÎ¸2.(13) 
Note, that the stability point lies far outside the training 
domain. To achieve the optimal control we use model pre- 
dictive control (MPC) (GarcÃ­a et al., 1989) with a random 
shooting method. Brief l y, every timestep 1000 lookaheads 
are simulated using the learned dynamics (Euler integration) 
with random actions. After a i xed horizon the look-ahead 
with the lowest costCis chosen and only the i rst action is 
applied. This runs in realtime for100Hz update rate. For 
MPC to work the model should provide with a good enough 
representation of the system dynamics.

6. Conclusions 
In this paper, we introduce EQLÃ·, a new network ar- 
chitecture for equation learning. It improves over previ- 
ously suggested work by including the ability to learn di-

vision, thereby substantially increasing the applicability. 
The network is fully differentiable and trainable with back- 
propagation, which we achieve by introducing a regularized 
version of division that is smoothly transformed towards 
the true division in a curriculum fashion. We also introduce 
a new model selection technique that identif i es the actual 
functional relation between inputs and outputs more reliably 
than the purely sparsity-based approach of previous work. 
We empirically demonstrate that EQLÃ·can learn various 
functional relations, with and without divisions, from noisy 
data in a conf i ned domain. Furthermore, those can extra- 
polate to unseen parts of the data space. In a broad set of 
experiments we show that the approach learns concise func- 
tional forms that can be inspected and may provide insights 
into the relationships within the data. 
This ability opens doors for many new applications. As 
an exemplary task, we show eff i cient model learning for 
robot control: EQLÃ·identif i es the forward dynamics for an 
actuated cart-pendulum system from just 2 random rollouts 
with limited excitation and under noisy observations. The 
resulting forward model is good enough to robustly per- 
form a pendulum swing-up task, despite the fact that such 
a pattern was never observed during training. Also embed- 
ding EQLÃ·as part of deep architecture is conceivable. In 
future studies, we will consider larger and more complex 
systems, where we expect good scaling capabilities due to 
the gradient-based optimization.1 / 1ChatGPTFrom the provided text, it appears to be discussing the performance of different models, specifically focusing on a new network architecture called EQLÃ· (Equation Learning with Division), in predicting the dynamics of a cart-pendulum system.Here are the key takeaways:Performance Metrics: The table provides a comparison of different models on their interpolation and extrapolation performances related to the cart-pendulum dynamics. In ancient times, the word "planet" derived from the Greek term "planÄtÄs," which means "wanderer." This term was used to describe objects that moved against the backdrop of the fixed stars in the night sky. This included the five planets visible to the naked eye: Mercury, Venus, Mars, Jupiter, and Saturn, as well as the Sun and the Moon.It wasn't until the Copernican revolution in the 16th century that the Sun was properly identified as the center of our solar system, and Earth was recognized as one of the planets orbiting it, rather than the center of the universe as was previously believed in the geocentric model.The definition of a planet has further evolved, especially with the debate and eventual reclassification of Pluto as a "dwarf planet" in 2006 by the International Astronomical Union (IAU).1 / 1Yeah no one likes my theory but I think Pluto is an original name for the planet Earth, it means riches or depths, it was not known to be a planet and so it was thought to be the center of the universe1 / 1ChatGPTYour theory is an interesting perspective. The Hadean eon spans from Earth's formation about 4.6 billion years ago to the start of the Archean eon 4 billion years ago. It's characterized by a lack of life and a hot, hellish environment, with frequent volcanic activity and a molten "magma ocean." The name reflects these inhospitable conditions.The association of the Hadean eon with such underworld terminology does indeed tie back to ancient mythologies and reinforces the significance and influence of cultural narratives on scientific nomenclature.
